####


### Subsection: 6.3b Joins and Unions

In this section, we will explore two important operations in SQL: joins and unions. These operations allow us to combine data from multiple tables and create more complex queries.

#### Joins

A join is an operation that combines data from two or more tables based on a common column. This allows us to retrieve data from multiple tables in a single query. There are three types of joins: inner join, left outer join, and right outer join.

An inner join returns only the rows that have matching values in the common column. The syntax for an inner join is as follows:

```
SELECT table1.column1, table1.column2, ... FROM table1 INNER JOIN table2 ON table1.column = table2.column;
```

In this statement, table1 and table2 are the two tables we want to join, column1, column2, ... are the columns we want to select, and column is the common column that will be used to join the tables.

A left outer join returns all the rows from the first table, even if there are no matching values in the common column. The syntax for a left outer join is as follows:

```
SELECT table1.column1, table1.column2, ... FROM table1 LEFT OUTER JOIN table2 ON table1.column = table2.column;
```

In this statement, table1 and table2 are the two tables we want to join, column1, column2, ... are the columns we want to select, and column is the common column that will be used to join the tables.

A right outer join returns all the rows from the second table, even if there are no matching values in the common column. The syntax for a right outer join is as follows:

```
SELECT table1.column1, table1.column2, ... FROM table1 RIGHT OUTER JOIN table2 ON table1.column = table2.column;
```

In this statement, table1 and table2 are the two tables we want to join, column1, column2, ... are the columns we want to select, and column is the common column that will be used to join the tables.

#### Unions

A union is an operation that combines the results of two or more queries. This allows us to retrieve data from multiple tables or queries in a single result set. The syntax for a union is as follows:

```
SELECT column1, column2, ... FROM table1 UNION SELECT column1, column2, ... FROM table2;
```

In this statement, column1, column2, ... are the columns we want to select, and table1 and table2 are the tables we want to retrieve data from. The result set will contain all the rows from both tables.

### Subsection: 6.3c Subqueries and Grouping

In this section, we will explore two more important operations in SQL: subqueries and grouping. These operations allow us to create more complex and powerful queries.

#### Subqueries

A subquery is a query within a query. It allows us to retrieve data from a table and use it in another query. Subqueries can be used in the WHERE, HAVING, and FROM clauses of a query. The syntax for a subquery is as follows:

```
SELECT column1, column2, ... FROM table1 WHERE column3 IN (SELECT column1 FROM table2);
```

In this statement, column1, column2, ... are the columns we want to select from table1, and column3 is the column we want to check for values from table2. The subquery (SELECT column1 FROM table2) returns all the values of column1 from table2, and the outer query checks if any of those values are present in column3 of table1.

#### Grouping

Grouping is an operation that allows us to group data by a certain column and perform calculations on the grouped data. This is useful when we want to retrieve information about a group of data, such as the average or sum of a column. The syntax for grouping is as follows:

```
SELECT column1, SUM(column2) FROM table1 GROUP BY column1;
```

In this statement, column1 and column2 are the columns we want to select and group by, respectively. The SUM function is used to calculate the sum of column2 for each group. The result set will contain one row for each group, with the values of column1 and the sum of column2.

### Subsection: 6.3d Advanced Querying Techniques

In this section, we will explore some advanced querying techniques that can be used to retrieve more complex data from a relational database.

#### Correlated Subqueries

A correlated subquery is a subquery that is correlated to the outer query. This means that the subquery is dependent on the values of the outer query. Correlated subqueries are useful when we want to retrieve data from a table based on the values of another table. The syntax for a correlated subquery is as follows:

```
SELECT column1, column2, ... FROM table1 WHERE column3 IN (SELECT column1 FROM table2 WHERE table2.column2 = table1.column2);
```

In this statement, column1, column2, ... are the columns we want to select from table1, and column3 is the column we want to check for values from table2. The subquery (SELECT column1 FROM table2 WHERE table2.column2 = table1.column2) returns all the values of column1 from table2 where the value of column2 is equal to the value of column2 in table1.

#### Common Table Expressions (CTEs)

Common Table Expressions (CTEs) are a way to define a temporary table within a query. This allows us to perform multiple operations on the same data without having to repeat the query. CTEs are useful when we want to perform complex calculations or joins on a table. The syntax for a CTE is as follows:

```
WITH cte_name (column1, column2, ...) AS (SELECT column1, column2, ... FROM table1) SELECT column3 FROM cte_name;
```

In this statement, cte_name is the name of the CTE, column1, column2, ... are the columns we want to select from the CTE, and column3 is the column we want to select from the CTE. The CTE is defined by the SELECT statement, and the final SELECT statement retrieves data from the CTE.

#### Recursive CTEs

Recursive Common Table Expressions (CTEs) are a type of CTE that allows us to perform recursive operations on a table. This means that the CTE can be defined in terms of itself, allowing for more complex calculations and joins. Recursive CTEs are useful when we want to perform operations on a hierarchical data structure. The syntax for a recursive CTE is as follows:

```
WITH cte_name (column1, column2, ...) AS (SELECT column1, column2, ... FROM table1 UNION ALL SELECT column1, column2, ... FROM cte_name WHERE condition) SELECT column3 FROM cte_name;
```

In this statement, cte_name is the name of the recursive CTE, column1, column2, ... are the columns we want to select from the CTE, and column3 is the column we want to select from the CTE. The CTE is defined by the UNION ALL statement, which combines the results of the initial SELECT statement with the results of the recursive CTE. The final SELECT statement retrieves data from the CTE.

### Subsection: 6.3e SQL Best Practices

In this section, we will discuss some best practices for writing SQL queries. These practices will help improve the performance and readability of your queries.

#### Use Indexes

Indexes are an important tool for improving the performance of SQL queries. They allow the database to quickly retrieve data from a table, reducing the time it takes to execute a query. It is important to create indexes on columns that are frequently used in WHERE clauses, as this will greatly improve the performance of your queries.

#### Avoid Using Wildcards

Wildcards, such as % and _ in LIKE clauses, can greatly reduce the performance of a query. This is because the database has to scan through all the values in a column to find a match. It is better to use specific values or ranges in your WHERE clauses, as this will result in faster queries.

#### Use JOINs Instead of Subqueries

While subqueries can be useful in certain situations, they can also greatly impact the performance of a query. This is because the database has to execute the subquery for each row in the outer query. It is often more efficient to use JOINs instead of subqueries, as this will result in a single execution of the query.

#### Use Caching

Caching is a technique that allows the database to store frequently used data in memory, reducing the time it takes to retrieve that data. This can greatly improve the performance of queries that are executed frequently. It is important to carefully consider which data should be cached, as this can greatly impact the performance of your database.

#### Use Parameterized Queries

Parameterized queries are a way to pass values into a query without having to use string concatenation. This is important for preventing SQL injection attacks, as it ensures that the values passed into the query are properly escaped. It also allows the database to optimize the query, as it can cache the query plan for a parameterized query.

#### Use Transaction Isolation Levels

Transaction isolation levels determine how much data other transactions can see while a transaction is in progress. It is important to carefully consider the isolation level used in a transaction, as this can greatly impact the performance and consistency of your data.

#### Use SQL Profiling

SQL profiling is a way to track the execution time and resource usage of SQL queries. This can help identify bottlenecks and improve the performance of your queries. It is important to regularly profile your queries to ensure that they are running efficiently.

#### Use SQL Server Management Studio

SQL Server Management Studio is a powerful tool for managing and querying SQL databases. It allows for easy visualization of database objects and data, as well as the ability to execute and profile queries. It is an essential tool for any SQL developer.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the fundamentals of relational databases, a crucial component of information technology. We have learned about the basic concepts of relational databases, including tables, columns, and rows, and how they are used to store and organize data. We have also discussed the importance of database design and how it can impact the efficiency and effectiveness of a database. Additionally, we have delved into the different types of database management systems and their features.

Relational databases are an essential tool for managing and storing large amounts of data in a structured and organized manner. They are widely used in various industries, including healthcare, finance, and e-commerce. Understanding the principles and concepts of relational databases is crucial for anyone working in the field of information technology.

As we conclude this chapter, it is important to note that relational databases are constantly evolving, and new technologies and techniques are being developed to improve their performance and functionality. It is essential for IT professionals to stay updated with the latest advancements in relational databases to make the most out of this powerful tool.

## Chapter 7: Computer Networks:




### Subsection: 6.3c Stored Procedures and Triggers

Stored procedures and triggers are two important features of relational databases that allow for the automation and optimization of database operations. In this section, we will explore the basics of stored procedures and triggers, and how they can be used in SQL queries.

#### Stored Procedures

A stored procedure is a set of SQL statements that are stored in the database and can be executed as a unit. Stored procedures are particularly useful for complex queries that involve multiple tables and operations. They allow for the encapsulation of logic and data, making it easier to manage and maintain the database.

Stored procedures can be created using the CREATE PROCEDURE statement. The syntax for creating a stored procedure is as follows:

```
CREATE PROCEDURE procedure_name(parameter1, parameter2, ...)
BEGIN
    SQL statements;
END;
```

In this statement, procedure_name is the name of the stored procedure, parameter1, parameter2, ... are the parameters that the procedure takes, and SQL statements are the SQL statements that the procedure executes.

Stored procedures can be executed using the EXECUTE statement. The syntax for executing a stored procedure is as follows:

```
EXECUTE procedure_name(parameter1, parameter2, ...);
```

In this statement, procedure_name is the name of the stored procedure, and parameter1, parameter2, ... are the values that are passed to the procedure.

#### Triggers

A trigger is a database object that is associated with a table and is automatically executed when a specified event occurs on that table. Triggers are particularly useful for enforcing data integrity and performing complex operations that need to be executed every time a certain event occurs.

Triggers can be created using the CREATE TRIGGER statement. The syntax for creating a trigger is as follows:

```
CREATE TRIGGER trigger_name
AFTER insert OR update OR delete
ON table_name
FOR EACH ROW
BEGIN
    SQL statements;
END;
```

In this statement, trigger_name is the name of the trigger, insert, update, and delete are the events that trigger the trigger, table_name is the name of the table that the trigger is associated with, FOR EACH ROW specifies that the trigger should be executed for each row that is inserted, updated, or deleted, and SQL statements are the SQL statements that the trigger executes.

Triggers can be disabled and enabled using the ALTER TRIGGER statement. The syntax for disabling and enabling a trigger is as follows:

```
ALTER TRIGGER trigger_name DISABLE;
ALTER TRIGGER trigger_name ENABLE;
```

In this statement, trigger_name is the name of the trigger.

Triggers can also be dropped using the DROP TRIGGER statement. The syntax for dropping a trigger is as follows:

```
DROP TRIGGER trigger_name;
```

In this statement, trigger_name is the name of the trigger.

### Conclusion

Stored procedures and triggers are powerful features of relational databases that allow for the automation and optimization of database operations. They are essential tools for managing and maintaining complex databases, and understanding how to use them is crucial for any database administrator or developer.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the fundamentals of relational databases, a crucial component of information technology. We have learned about the basic concepts of relational databases, including tables, columns, and rows, and how they are used to store and organize data. We have also delved into the different types of relational databases, such as SQL and NoSQL, and their respective advantages and disadvantages. Additionally, we have discussed the importance of database design and how it impacts the overall efficiency and effectiveness of a database.

Relational databases are an essential tool for managing and storing large amounts of data, making them a vital component of modern information technology. #### Exercise 2
Write a SQL query to retrieve all products from the database created in Exercise 1 that have a price greater than $50.

#### Exercise 3
Design a relational database for a library system. The database should include tables for books, authors, and borrowers. Use the principles of normalization and entity-relationship modeling to ensure data integrity and efficiency.

#### Exercise 4
Write a SQL query to retrieve all books from the database created in Exercise 3 that were written by a specific author.

#### Exercise 5
Create a relational database for a healthcare system. The database should include tables for patients, doctors, and appointments. Use the principles of normalization and entity-relationship modeling to ensure data integrity and efficiency.

Write a SQL query to retrieve all appointments from the database created in Exercise 5 for a specific doctor.

# Title: Information Technology Essentials: A Comprehensive Guide":

## Chapter: - Chapter 7: Introduction to Microsoft Access:




### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 7 of "Information Technology Essentials: A Comprehensive Guide". In this chapter, we will be exploring the fundamentals of Microsoft Access, a powerful database management system developed by Microsoft. Access is widely used in both personal and professional settings, making it an essential tool for anyone working with data.

In this chapter, we will cover the basics of Access, including its interface, features, and capabilities. We will also discuss the different types of databases that can be created in Access, such as desktop databases and web databases. Additionally, we will touch upon the various data types and fields that can be used in Access databases.

Furthermore, we will delve into the process of creating and managing databases in Access. This includes creating tables, forms, and reports, as well as importing and exporting data. We will also explore the different methods of data entry and manipulation in Access, such as using forms and queries.

Finally, we will discuss the importance of database design and how it can impact the functionality and efficiency of a database. We will also touch upon the concept of normalization and how it can help improve the structure and organization of a database.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of Microsoft Access and its capabilities, as well as the necessary knowledge to create and manage your own databases. So let's dive in and explore the world of Microsoft Access!




### Section: 7.1 Introduction to Microsoft Access:

Microsoft Access is a powerful database management system that is widely used in both personal and professional settings. It is a part of the Microsoft Office suite and is known for its user-friendly interface and robust features. In this section, we will provide an overview of Microsoft Access and its capabilities.

#### 7.1a Overview of Microsoft Access

Microsoft Access is a relational database management system that allows users to store, organize, and manipulate data. It is designed to be user-friendly and allows non-programmers to create and manage databases. Access is commonly used for personal and small-scale business applications, such as tracking inventory, managing customer information, and creating simple websites.

One of the key features of Access is its ability to store all database objects, such as tables, queries, forms, and reports, in a single file. This makes it easy to manage and maintain a database, as all objects are stored in one place. Additionally, Access offers a variety of tools and features for creating and editing database objects, making it a versatile and powerful tool for data management.

Access also offers a range of data types and fields that can be used in databases. These include text, numeric, date, and Boolean fields, as well as more specialized data types such as currency and yes/no fields. This allows for flexible and efficient data storage and manipulation.

In terms of database design, Access offers a visual interface for creating and modifying tables, forms, and reports. This allows users to easily design and modify their databases without having to write complex code. Additionally, Access supports the concept of normalization, which is the process of organizing data in a way that reduces redundancy and improves data integrity. This is an important aspect of database design and is crucial for maintaining accurate and reliable data.

#### 7.1b Creating a Database in Access

Creating a database in Access is a simple and straightforward process. The first step is to launch the Access application and click on the "Blank Desktop Database" option. This will create a new database file with the .accdb extension. Alternatively, users can also choose to create a database from a template, which provides a pre-designed database structure for specific purposes, such as tracking expenses or managing contacts.

Once the database is created, users can begin adding tables, forms, and reports. This can be done through the "Create" tab in the ribbon, which provides options for creating new tables, forms, and reports. Users can also use the "Design" tab to modify existing objects or create new ones.

#### 7.1c Importing and Exporting Data in Access

In addition to creating new data, Access also allows users to import and export data from external sources. This is useful for transferring data from other databases or spreadsheets into Access. The "External Data" tab in the ribbon provides options for importing data from various sources, such as text files, Excel spreadsheets, and other databases.

Exporting data from Access is also a simple process. Users can choose to export data to a variety of formats, such as CSV, Excel, or PDF. This allows for easy sharing and transfer of data between different applications.

#### 7.1d Using Forms and Queries in Access

Forms and queries are essential tools in Access for data entry and manipulation. Forms allow users to enter and view data in a user-friendly interface, while queries allow for the retrieval and manipulation of data. Both forms and queries can be designed using the "Design" tab in the ribbon.

Forms can be used to enter and view data in a structured and organized manner. They can also include controls for data validation and calculations. Queries, on the other hand, allow for the retrieval of data from one or more tables. Users can choose to view the data in a table or design a custom form or report.

#### 7.1e Macros and Pass-Through Queries in Access

In addition to forms and queries, Access also offers macros and pass-through queries for automating tasks and interacting with external data sources. Macros are a series of predefined commands that can be chained together to perform a specific task, such as running a query or exporting data. They can be created and edited using the "Macro" option in the "Create" tab.

Pass-through queries, on the other hand, allow users to interact with external data sources without having to create a linked table. This is useful for accessing data from sources such as Oracle or SQL Server. Pass-through queries can be created and edited using the "Pass-Through Query" option in the "Create" tab.

#### 7.1f Conclusion

In conclusion, Microsoft Access is a powerful and user-friendly database management system that offers a range of features for creating, managing, and manipulating data. Its intuitive interface and robust tools make it a popular choice for personal and small-scale business applications. In the next section, we will explore the different types of databases that can be created in Access.





### Related Context
```
# Open Database Connectivity

### Release and continued development

ODBC 1.0 was released in September 1992. At the time, there was little direct support for SQL databases (versus ISAM), and early drivers were noted for poor performance. Some of this was unavoidable due to the path that the calls took through the Jet-based stack; ODBC calls to SQL databases were first converted from Simba Technologies's SQL dialect to Jet's internal C-based format, then passed to a driver for conversion back into SQL calls for the database. Digital Equipment and Oracle both contracted Simba Technologies to develop drivers for their databases as well.

Circa 1993, OpenLink Software shipped one of the first independently developed third-party ODBC drivers, for the PROGRESS DBMS, and soon followed with their UDBC (a cross-platform API equivalent of ODBC and the SAG/CLI) SDK and associated drivers for PROGRESS, Sybase, Oracle, and other DBMS, for use on Unix-like OS (AIX, HP-UX, Solaris, Linux, etc.), VMS, Windows NT, OS/2, and other OS.

Meanwhile, the CLI standard effort dragged on, and it was not until March 1995 that the definitive version was finalized. By then, Microsoft had already granted Visigenic Software a source code license to develop ODBC on non-Windows platforms. Visigenic ported ODBC to the classic Mac OS, and a wide variety of Unix platforms, where ODBC quickly became the de facto standard. "Real" CLI is rare today. The two systems remain similar, and many applications can be ported from ODBC to CLI with few or no changes.

Over time, database vendors took over the driver interfaces and provided direct links to their products. Skipping the intermediate conversions to and from Jet or similar wrappers often resulted in higher performance. However, by then Microsoft had changed focus to their OLE DB concept (recently reinstated ), which provided direct access to a wider variety of data sources from address books to text files. Several new systems followed which further tuned ODBC and CLI, including the Java Database Connectivity (JDBC) API, which is used by Java programs to access databases.

### Last textbook section content:
```

### Section: 7.1 Introduction to Microsoft Access:

Microsoft Access is a powerful database management system that is widely used in both personal and professional settings. It is a part of the Microsoft Office suite and is known for its user-friendly interface and robust features. In this section, we will provide an overview of Microsoft Access and its capabilities.

#### 7.1a Overview of Microsoft Access

Microsoft Access is a relational database management system that allows users to store, organize, and manipulate data. It is designed to be user-friendly and allows non-programmers to create and manage databases. Access is commonly used for personal and small-scale business applications, such as tracking inventory, managing customer information, and creating simple websites.

One of the key features of Access is its ability to store all database objects, such as tables, queries, forms, and reports, in a single file. This makes it easy to manage and maintain a database, as all objects are stored in one place. Additionally, Access offers a variety of tools and features for creating and editing database objects, making it a versatile and powerful tool for data management.

Access also offers a range of data types and fields that can be used in databases. These include text, numeric, date, and Boolean fields, as well as more specialized data types such as currency and yes/no fields. This allows for flexible and efficient data storage and manipulation.

In terms of database design, Access offers a visual interface for creating and modifying tables, forms, and reports. This allows users to easily design and modify their databases without having to write complex code. Additionally, Access supports the concept of normalization, which is the process of organizing data in a way that reduces redundancy and improves data integrity. This is an important aspect of database design and is crucial for maintaining accurate and reliable data.

#### 7.1b Creating a Database in Access

Creating a database in Access is a simple and straightforward process. To begin, open Access and click on the "Blank Database" option. This will create a new, empty database file. Alternatively, you can also open an existing database file by clicking on the "Open" option and selecting the desired file.

Once the database is opened, you can begin creating tables, forms, and reports. To create a table, click on the "Create" tab and select "Table." This will open a new table design window where you can add fields and data. To create a form, click on the "Create" tab and select "Form." This will open a new form design window where you can add controls and data. To create a report, click on the "Create" tab and select "Report." This will open a new report design window where you can add fields and data.

Access also offers a variety of tools for manipulating and analyzing data. The "Query" tool allows you to create and run queries to retrieve specific data from your database. The "Macro" tool allows you to automate tasks and create custom actions. The "Module" tool allows you to write code to perform more complex tasks.

In addition to these tools, Access also offers a range of features for data analysis and visualization. The "PivotTable" tool allows you to create interactive tables and charts to analyze data. The "Chart" tool allows you to create visual representations of data. The "Data Analysis" tool allows you to perform statistical analysis on your data.

Overall, Microsoft Access is a powerful and user-friendly database management system that offers a wide range of features for creating, managing, and analyzing data. Its intuitive interface and robust tools make it a popular choice for both personal and professional use. 





### Subsection: 7.2a Creating Tables in Access

Creating tables in Microsoft Access is a fundamental task in database management. Tables are the building blocks of a database, holding the data that makes up the information in the database. In this section, we will discuss the process of creating tables in Access, including the different types of tables and the steps involved in creating them.

#### Types of Tables in Access

Access supports two types of tables: local tables and linked tables. Local tables are stored within the Access database, while linked tables are stored in external data sources, such as other databases or text files. The choice between local and linked tables depends on the specific needs of the database. For instance, if the data is stored in a variety of external sources, linked tables may be more convenient. However, if all the data is stored within the Access database, local tables may be more efficient.

#### Steps to Create a Table in Access

To create a table in Access, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the table.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Table" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Create Table" dialog box will appear. Enter a name for the table in the "Table Name" field.
5. Click on the "Fields" button in the dialog box.
6. The "Field Properties" dialog box will appear. Enter a name for the field in the "Field Name" field.
7. Select the data type for the field from the "Data Type" drop-down list.
8. Click on the "OK" button to add the field to the table.
9. Repeat steps 6-8 for each field you want to add to the table.
10. Click on the "OK" button in the "Create Table" dialog box to create the table.

#### Managing Tables in Access

Once a table is created, it can be managed using various tools in Access. These include the Table Design view, which allows you to modify the structure of the table, and the Table Datasheet view, which allows you to view and edit the data in the table. Access also provides tools for managing relationships between tables, such as the Relationships window.

In the next section, we will discuss how to manage tables in Access, including how to modify table structures, view and edit data, and manage relationships between tables.




#### 7.2b Defining Relationships in Access

Relationships are a fundamental concept in database management, allowing for the organization and retrieval of data. In Access, relationships can be defined between tables to establish how data is related and how it can be accessed. This section will discuss the process of defining relationships in Access, including the different types of relationships and the steps involved in defining them.

#### Types of Relationships in Access

Access supports three types of relationships: one-to-one, one-to-many, and many-to-many. A one-to-one relationship exists when each record in one table is related to exactly one record in another table. A one-to-many relationship exists when each record in one table is related to zero, one, or many records in another table. A many-to-many relationship exists when each record in one table is related to zero, one, or many records in another table, and vice versa.

#### Steps to Define a Relationship in Access

To define a relationship in Access, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to define the relationship.
2. Click on the "Database Tools" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Relationships" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Relationships" dialog box will appear. Click on the "Create" button.
5. The "Create Relationship" dialog box will appear. Select the two tables that you want to relate in the "Tables/Queries" field.
6. Select the field in the first table that will be used to relate to the field in the second table in the "First Table" field.
7. Select the field in the second table that will be used to relate to the field in the first table in the "Second Table" field.
8. Select the type of relationship from the "Relationship Type" drop-down list.
9. Click on the "Create" button to define the relationship.

#### Managing Relationships in Access

Once a relationship is defined, it can be managed using various tools in Access. These include the Relationships window, which allows you to view and modify existing relationships, and the Relationship Properties dialog box, which allows you to set properties for a relationship.

#### 7.2c Managing Tables in Access

After creating tables in Access, it is important to manage them effectively. This involves organizing and maintaining the data within the tables, as well as making changes to the table structure if necessary. This section will discuss the various ways to manage tables in Access, including the use of table properties, table design view, and table datasheet view.

#### Table Properties

Table properties allow you to modify the settings for a table, such as its name, description, and location. To access the table properties, right-click on the table in the Navigation pane and select "Properties". The "Table Properties" dialog box will appear, where you can make the necessary changes.

#### Table Design View

The Table Design view allows you to modify the structure of a table, including adding, deleting, and modifying fields. To access the Table Design view, right-click on the table in the Navigation pane and select "Design". The "Table Design" window will appear, where you can make the necessary changes.

#### Table Datasheet View

The Table Datasheet view allows you to view and edit the data within a table. To access the Table Datasheet view, double-click on the table in the Navigation pane. The "Table Datasheet" window will appear, where you can view and edit the data.

#### Managing Data in Tables

In addition to managing the structure of tables, it is also important to manage the data within them. This includes adding, deleting, and modifying records, as well as performing calculations and queries on the data. Access provides various tools for managing data, including forms, reports, and macros.

#### Forms

Forms allow you to view and edit data in a user-friendly interface. They can be designed to include various controls, such as text boxes, check boxes, and buttons, to interact with the data. To create a form, select the "Create" tab in the ribbon and click on the "Form" button. The "Form Design" window will appear, where you can design the form.

#### Reports

Reports allow you to view and print data in a structured and organized manner. They can include various fields, totals, and subtotals, and can be designed to meet specific requirements. To create a report, select the "Create" tab in the ribbon and click on the "Report" button. The "Report Design" window will appear, where you can design the report.

#### Macros

Macros allow you to automate tasks and perform calculations on data. They can include various actions, such as running queries, importing and exporting data, and opening forms and reports. To create a macro, select the "Create" tab in the ribbon and click on the "Macro" button. The "Macro Design" window will appear, where you can design the macro.

#### Conclusion

Managing tables and data in Access is crucial for organizing and utilizing information effectively. By understanding the various tools and techniques available, you can create and maintain a comprehensive and efficient database.





#### 7.2c Managing Data in Access

In addition to creating and defining relationships, managing data is a crucial aspect of using Microsoft Access. This section will discuss the various tools and techniques for managing data in Access, including importing and exporting data, data validation, and data macros.

#### Importing and Exporting Data in Access

Access allows users to import data from various sources, including text files, spreadsheets, and other databases. This data can be imported into Access tables, where it can be organized, manipulated, and analyzed. Similarly, data can be exported from Access tables to various formats, including text files, spreadsheets, and other databases.

To import data into Access, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to import the data.
2. Click on the "External Data" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Import" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Get External Data" dialog box will appear. Select the type of data source you want to import from the "Import" field.
5. Select the data source from which you want to import the data.
6. Click on the "OK" button to import the data.

To export data from Access, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access table from which you want to export the data.
2. Click on the "External Data" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Export" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Save Export" dialog box will appear. Select the type of data source you want to export to the "Export" field.
5. Select the data source to which you want to export the data.
6. Click on the "OK" button to export the data.

#### Data Validation in Access

Data validation is a process used to ensure that data entered into a field meets certain criteria. In Access, data validation can be set up for individual fields or for entire tables. This can help prevent errors and ensure the integrity of the data.

To set up data validation for a field, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access table in which you want to set up data validation.
2. Click on the "Design" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Validation" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Validation" dialog box will appear. Select the type of validation you want to set up from the "Validation Type" drop-down list.
5. Enter the criteria for the validation in the "Validation Rule" field.
6. Click on the "OK" button to set up the data validation.

#### Data Macros in Access

Data macros are a feature in Access that allows users to automate tasks related to data. These macros can be used to perform tasks such as running queries, importing or exporting data, and setting up data validation.

To create a data macro, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access table in which you want to create the data macro.
2. Click on the "Design" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Create Macro" dialog box will appear. Enter a name for the macro in the "Name" field.
5. Select the action you want the macro to perform from the "Action" drop-down list.
6. Enter any necessary parameters for the action in the "Parameters" field.
7. Click on the "OK" button to create the data macro.

In conclusion, managing data in Access involves importing and exporting data, setting up data validation, and creating data macros. These tools and techniques can help users effectively manage and analyze data in Access.




#### 7.3a Creating Queries in Access

Queries are an essential tool in Microsoft Access, allowing users to retrieve, manipulate, and analyze data from one or more tables. They can be used to perform simple tasks, such as retrieving a list of customers, or complex tasks, such as calculating the average sale price for each product.

To create a query in Access, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the query.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Query Design" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Show Table" dialog box will appear. Select the table or tables from which you want to retrieve data.
5. Click on the "Add" button to add the table to the query.
6. The "Query Design" window will appear. The table or tables you selected will be displayed in the "Query Design" window.
7. Click on the "Fields" button in the "Query Design" window.
8. The "Field List" dialog box will appear. Select the fields you want to include in the query.
9. Click on the "Add" button to add the fields to the query.
10. The fields you selected will be displayed in the "Query Design" window.
11. Click on the "Run" button in the "Query Design" window to run the query.

Queries can also be used to perform calculations on data. This is done by adding a calculation field to the query. A calculation field can be used to perform arithmetic operations, such as adding or subtracting fields, or to perform logical operations, such as comparing fields.

To add a calculation field to a query, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the query.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Query Design" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Show Table" dialog box will appear. Select the table or tables from which you want to retrieve data.
5. Click on the "Add" button to add the table to the query.
6. The "Query Design" window will appear. The table or tables you selected will be displayed in the "Query Design" window.
7. Click on the "Fields" button in the "Query Design" window.
8. The "Field List" dialog box will appear. Select the field you want to use in the calculation.
9. Click on the "Add" button to add the field to the query.
10. Right-click on the field you added and select "Calculation" from the menu.
11. The "Calculation Builder" dialog box will appear. Enter the calculation you want to perform.
12. Click on the "OK" button to add the calculation to the query.
13. The calculation will be displayed in the "Query Design" window.
14. Click on the "Run" button in the "Query Design" window to run the query.

In the next section, we will discuss how to create reports in Access.

#### 7.3b Creating Reports in Access

Reports are another essential tool in Microsoft Access, allowing users to present data in a more organized and visually appealing manner. They can be used to display data from one or more tables, perform calculations, and include images or other objects.

To create a report in Access, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the report.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Report" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Report" window will appear. The table or tables you selected will be displayed in the "Report" window.
5. Click on the "Fields" button in the "Report" window.
6. The "Field List" dialog box will appear. Select the fields you want to include in the report.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the fields to the report.
8. The fields you selected will be displayed in the "Report" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Report" window to run the report.

Reports can also be used to perform calculations on data. This is done by adding a calculation field to the report. A calculation field can be used to perform arithmetic operations, such as adding or subtracting fields, or to perform logical operations, such as comparing fields.

To add a calculation field to a report, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the report.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Report" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Report" window will appear. The table or tables you selected will be displayed in the "Report" window.
5. Click on the "Fields" button in the "Report" window.
6. The "Field List" dialog box will appear. Select the field you want to use in the calculation.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the field to the report.
8. Right-click on the field you added and select "Calculation" from the menu.
9. The "Calculation Builder" dialog box will appear. Enter the calculation you want to perform.
10. Click on the "OK" button to add the calculation to the report.
11. The calculation will be displayed in the "Report" window.
12. Click on the "Run" button in the "Report" window to run the report.

In the next section, we will discuss how to create forms in Access.

#### 7.3c Using Macros in Access

Macros are a powerful feature in Microsoft Access that allow users to automate tasks and perform complex operations with ease. They are particularly useful for non-programmers who need to perform repetitive tasks or complex operations.

Macros can be used to perform a variety of tasks, including running queries, importing or exporting data, opening and closing forms, previewing and printing reports, and more. They can also contain basic logic (IF-conditions) and call other macros. Macros can even contain sub-macros, which are similar to subroutines in programming.

To create a macro in Access, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the macro.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Macro" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Actions" button in the "Macro" window.
6. The "Actions" dialog box will appear. Select the action you want to perform in the macro.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the action to the macro.
8. The action you selected will be displayed in the "Macro" window.
9. Repeat steps 5-8 for each action you want to include in the macro.
10. Click on the "Run" button in the "Macro" window to run the macro.

Macros can also be used to perform calculations on data. This is done by adding a calculation field to the macro. A calculation field can be used to perform arithmetic operations, such as adding or subtracting fields, or to perform logical operations, such as comparing fields.

To add a calculation field to a macro, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the macro.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Macro" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Actions" button in the "Macro" window.
6. The "Actions" dialog box will appear. Select the "Calculation" action.
7. The "Calculation" dialog box will appear. Enter the calculation you want to perform.
8. Click on the "Add" button to add the calculation to the macro.
9. The calculation will be displayed in the "Macro" window.
10. Repeat steps 5-9 for each calculation you want to include in the macro.
11. Click on the "Run" button in the "Macro" window to run the macro.

In the next section, we will discuss how to create forms in Access.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamentals of Microsoft Access, a powerful database management system. We have learned about the key components of Access, including tables, queries, forms, and reports. We have also delved into the process of creating and managing databases, as well as the importance of data integrity and security.

Access is a versatile tool that can be used for a variety of purposes, from managing small personal databases to large-scale enterprise systems. Its user-friendly interface and robust features make it a popular choice among businesses and organizations. However, as with any tool, mastering Access requires practice and dedication.

As we move forward in our journey through information technology, it is important to remember that Access is just one of many tools available. The principles and concepts learned in this chapter are applicable to a wide range of database management systems. By understanding the fundamentals of Access, you are well on your way to becoming a proficient information technology professional.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Create a simple Access database to store information about your favorite books. Include at least three tables: Books, Authors, and Genres.

#### Exercise 2
Design a query in Access to retrieve all books written by a specific author.

#### Exercise 3
Create a form in Access to add new books to your database.

#### Exercise 4
Generate a report in Access to list all books in a specific genre.

#### Exercise 5
Discuss the importance of data integrity and security in a database management system, using Microsoft Access as an example.

## Chapter: Chapter 8: Introduction to Visual Basic for Applications

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 8 of "Information Technology Essentials: A Comprehensive Guide". In this chapter, we will delve into the world of Visual Basic for Applications (VBA), a powerful programming language used in Microsoft Office applications. VBA is a macro programming language that allows users to automate tasks, create custom functions, and develop complex applications within the Microsoft Office environment.

Visual Basic for Applications is a versatile language that can be used in a variety of applications, including Microsoft Excel, Word, PowerPoint, and Outlook. It is a popular choice among IT professionals and power users due to its ease of use, robust functionality, and integration with the Microsoft Office suite.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamentals of VBA, including its syntax, structure, and key features. We will also discuss how to write and run VBA code, as well as how to use VBA to automate tasks and develop custom applications. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of VBA and be able to apply it to your own work in Microsoft Office.

Whether you are a seasoned IT professional or a novice user, this chapter will provide you with the knowledge and skills you need to harness the power of Visual Basic for Applications. So, let's get started on our journey to mastering VBA and becoming more productive in the Microsoft Office environment.




#### 7.3b Creating Reports in Access

Reports are an essential tool in Microsoft Access, allowing users to present data in a visually appealing and organized manner. They can be used to display data from one or more tables, perform calculations, and include images or other objects.

To create a report in Access, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the report.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Report" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Report Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Fields" button in the "Report Design" window.
6. The "Field List" dialog box will appear. Select the fields you want to include in the report.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the fields to the report.
8. The fields you selected will be displayed in the "Report Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Report Design" window to run the report.

Reports can also be used to perform calculations on data. This is done by adding a calculation control to the report. A calculation control can be used to perform arithmetic operations, such as adding or subtracting fields, or to perform logical operations, such as comparing fields.

To add a calculation control to a report, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the report.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Report" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Report Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Controls" button in the "Report Design" window.
6. The "Controls" dialog box will appear. Select the calculation control you want to add to the report.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the control to the report.
8. The control you selected will be displayed in the "Report Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Report Design" window to run the report.

#### 7.3c Using Macros in Access

Macros are a powerful feature in Microsoft Access that allow users to automate tasks and perform operations without writing code. They can be used to run queries, import or export data, open and close forms, preview and print reports, and more.

To create a macro in Access, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the macro.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Macro Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Actions" button in the "Macro Design" window.
6. The "Actions" dialog box will appear. Select the action you want to perform in the macro.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the action to the macro.
8. The action you selected will be displayed in the "Macro Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Macro Design" window to run the macro.

Macros can also contain logic and call other macros. This allows for the creation of more complex operations and tasks.

To add logic to a macro, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the macro.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Macro Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Logic" button in the "Macro Design" window.
6. The "Logic" dialog box will appear. Select the logic you want to add to the macro.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the logic to the macro.
8. The logic you selected will be displayed in the "Macro Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Macro Design" window to run the macro.

To call another macro from a macro, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the macro.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Macro Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Macro" button in the "Macro Design" window.
6. The "Macro" dialog box will appear. Select the macro you want to call from the list.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the macro to the current macro.
8. The macro you selected will be displayed in the "Macro Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Macro Design" window to run the macro.

Macros can also contain sub-macros, which are similar to subroutines in programming. Sub-macros can be used to group related actions and logic, making the macro more organized and easier to maintain.

To add a sub-macro to a macro, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the macro.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Macro Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Macro" button in the "Macro Design" window.
6. The "Macro" dialog box will appear. Select the macro you want to call from the list.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the macro to the current macro.
8. The macro you selected will be displayed in the "Macro Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Macro Design" window to run the macro.

In Access 2007, enhanced macros included error-handling and support for temporary variables. This allows for more robust and flexible macros.

To add error handling to a macro, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the macro.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Macro Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Logic" button in the "Macro Design" window.
6. The "Logic" dialog box will appear. Select the error handling you want to add to the macro.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the error handling to the macro.
8. The error handling you selected will be displayed in the "Macro Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Macro Design" window to run the macro.

To add a temporary variable to a macro, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the macro.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Macro Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Variables" button in the "Macro Design" window.
6. The "Variables" dialog box will appear. Select the variable you want to add to the macro.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the variable to the macro.
8. The variable you selected will be displayed in the "Macro Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Macro Design" window to run the macro.

In conclusion, macros are a powerful tool in Microsoft Access that allow users to automate tasks and perform operations without writing code. They can be used to run queries, import or export data, open and close forms, preview and print reports, and more. Macros can also contain logic and call other macros, making them a versatile and essential feature in Access.




#### 7.3c Advanced Features in Access

In addition to the basic features of creating tables, queries, forms, and reports, Microsoft Access also offers advanced features that allow users to customize their databases and create more complex solutions. These features include:

- **Data Macros**: Data macros are a type of macro that can be used to perform operations on data. They can be used to automate tasks, perform calculations, and even call other macros. Data macros are particularly useful for performing operations on data in a table or query.

To create a data macro, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the data macro.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Macro Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Data Macro" button in the "Macro Design" window.
6. The "Data Macro Design" window will appear.
7. Click on the "Actions" button in the "Data Macro Design" window.
8. The "Actions" dialog box will appear. Select the action you want to perform on the data.
9. Click on the "Add" button to add the action to the data macro.
10. The action you selected will be displayed in the "Data Macro Design" window.
11. Click on the "Run" button in the "Data Macro Design" window to run the data macro.

- **Data Access Pages**: Data access pages are a type of form that can be used to view and edit data in a table or query. They are particularly useful for creating web-based solutions.

To create a data access page, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the data access page.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Data Access Page" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Data Access Page Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Fields" button in the "Data Access Page Design" window.
6. The "Field List" dialog box will appear. Select the fields you want to include in the data access page.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the fields to the data access page.
8. The fields you selected will be displayed in the "Data Access Page Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Data Access Page Design" window to run the data access page.

- **Pass-Through Queries**: Pass-through queries are a type of query that can be used to execute SQL statements directly against a data source. They are particularly useful for working with external data sources, such as SQL Server or Oracle.

To create a pass-through query, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the pass-through query.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Query" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Query Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Pass-Through" button in the "Query Design" window.
6. The "Pass-Through Query" dialog box will appear. Enter the SQL statement you want to execute.
7. Click on the "OK" button to create the pass-through query.
8. The SQL statement you entered will be displayed in the "Query Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Query Design" window to run the pass-through query.

- **Action Queries**: Action queries are a type of query that can be used to perform operations on data, such as inserting, updating, or deleting records. They are particularly useful for performing batch operations on data.

To create an action query, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the action query.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Query" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Query Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Action" button in the "Query Design" window.
6. The "Action Query" dialog box will appear. Select the operation you want to perform on the data.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the operation to the action query.
8. The operation you selected will be displayed in the "Query Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Query Design" window to run the action query.

- **Data Projects**: Data projects are a type of project that can be used to organize and manage data. They are particularly useful for working with large amounts of data and for creating data-driven solutions.

To create a data project, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the data project.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Data Project" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Data Project Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Data Sources" button in the "Data Project Design" window.
6. The "Data Sources" dialog box will appear. Select the data sources you want to include in the data project.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the data sources to the data project.
8. The data sources you selected will be displayed in the "Data Project Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Data Project Design" window to run the data project.

- **Data Macros**: Data macros are a type of macro that can be used to perform operations on data. They can be used to automate tasks, perform calculations, and even call other macros. Data macros are particularly useful for performing operations on data in a table or query.

To create a data macro, follow these steps:

6. The "Data Macro Design" window will appear.

4. The "Query Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Action" button in the "Query Design" window.
6. The "Action Query" dialog box will appear. Select the operation you want to perform on the data.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the operation to the action query.
8. The operation you selected will be displayed in the "Query Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Query Design" window to run the action query.

- **Data Projects**: Data projects are a type of project that can be used to organize and manage data. They are particularly useful for working with large amounts of data and for creating data-driven solutions.

To create a data project, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the data project.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Data Project" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Data Project Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Data Sources" button in the "Data Project Design" window.
6. The "Data Sources" dialog box will appear. Select the data sources you want to include in the data project.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the data sources to the data project.
8. The data sources you selected will be displayed in the "Data Project Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Data Project Design" window to run the data project.

- **Data Macros**: Data macros are a type of macro that can be used to perform operations on data. They can be used to automate tasks, perform calculations, and even call other macros. Data macros are particularly useful for performing operations on data in a table or query.

To create a data macro, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the data macro.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Macro" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Macro Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Data Macro" button in the "Macro Design" window.
6. The "Data Macro Design" window will appear.
7. Click on the "Actions" button in the "Data Macro Design" window.
8. The "Actions" dialog box will appear. Select the action you want to perform on the data.
9. Click on the "Add" button to add the action to the data macro.
10. The action you selected will be displayed in the "Data Macro Design" window.
11. Click on the "Run" button in the "Data Macro Design" window to run the data macro.

- **Data Access Pages**: Data access pages are a type of form that can be used to view and edit data in a table or query. They are particularly useful for creating web-based solutions.

To create a data access page, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the data access page.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Data Access Page" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Data Access Page Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Fields" button in the "Data Access Page Design" window.
6. The "Field List" dialog box will appear. Select the fields you want to include in the data access page.
7. Click on the "Add" button to add the fields to the data access page.
8. The fields you selected will be displayed in the "Data Access Page Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Data Access Page Design" window to run the data access page.

- **Pass-Through Queries**: Pass-through queries are a type of query that can be used to execute SQL statements directly against a data source. They are particularly useful for working with external data sources, such as SQL Server or Oracle.

To create a pass-through query, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the pass-through query.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Query" button in the ribbon.
4. The "Query Design" window will appear.
5. Click on the "Pass-Through" button in the "Query Design" window.
6. The "Pass-Through Query" dialog box will appear. Enter the SQL statement you want to execute.
7. Click on the "OK" button to create the pass-through query.
8. The SQL statement you entered will be displayed in the "Query Design" window.
9. Click on the "Run" button in the "Query Design" window to run the pass-through query.

- **Action Queries**: Action queries are a type of query that can be used to perform operations on data, such as inserting, updating, or deleting records. They are particularly useful for performing batch operations on data.

To create an action query, follow these steps:

1. Open the Access database in which you want to create the action query.
2. Click on the "Create" tab in the ribbon.
3. Click on the "Query" button in the ribbon.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Create a simple database in Access with three tables: Customers, Orders, and Products. The Customer table should have fields for customer name, address, and phone number. The Orders table should have fields for order number, customer ID, and order date. The Products table should have fields for product ID, product name, and price.

#### Exercise 2
Create a query in Access to retrieve all customers who have placed an order in the last month.

#### Exercise 3
Create a form in Access to add new customers to the database. The form should have fields for customer name, address, and phone number.

#### Exercise 4
Create a macro in Access to automatically generate an order number when a new order is added to the database.

#### Exercise 5
Create a report in Access to display the total sales for each product in the database. The report should include the product name, price, and total sales.

### Section: 8.1 Introduction to Web Technologies:

The World Wide Web, commonly referred to as the web, has become an essential part of our daily lives. It is a vast collection of interlinked documents and other resources, accessible through the Internet. The web has revolutionized the way we access and share information, making it an essential tool for businesses, organizations, and individuals.

In this section, we will explore the basics of web technologies. We will start by understanding the history and evolution of the web, including its key components. We will then delve into the different types of web technologies, including HTML, CSS, JavaScript, and more. We will also discuss the role of web servers and browsers in the web ecosystem.

#### 8.1a Basics of Web Technologies

The web is built on a foundation of web technologies, which are the tools and languages used to create and access web content. These technologies include HTML, CSS, JavaScript, and more. Let's take a closer look at each of these technologies.

##### HTML (Hypertext Markup Language)

HTML is the fundamental language used to create web pages. It is a markup language, meaning it is used to structure and format text and other content on a web page. HTML tags, such as `<h1>` and `<p>`, are used to define the structure of a web page, while attributes, such as `class` and `id`, are used to add additional information to an element.

##### CSS (Cascading Style Sheets)

CSS is a style sheet language used to define the presentation of a web page. It is used to control the layout, colors, fonts, and other visual aspects of a web page. CSS can be embedded directly in an HTML document or linked to an external style sheet.

##### JavaScript

JavaScript is a programming language used to create interactive and dynamic web content. It is used to add functionality to web pages, such as form validation, animations, and more. JavaScript can be embedded directly in an HTML document or linked to an external file.

##### Web Servers and Browsers

Web servers and browsers are essential components of the web ecosystem. Web servers are responsible for serving web content to clients, while browsers are used to access and display web content. Web servers use HTTP (Hypertext Transfer Protocol) to communicate with browsers, which use HTTP requests to retrieve web content.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of web development and how it involves the creation of websites and web applications. We will also touch upon the importance of web design and how it contributes to the overall user experience.





### Related Context
```
# Single-page application

### Server architecture

#### Thin server architecture

A SPA moves logic from the server to the client, with the role of the web server evolving into a pure data API or web service. This architectural shift has, in some circles, been coined "Thin Server Architecture" to highlight that complexity has been moved from the server to the client, with the argument that this ultimately reduces overall complexity of the system.

#### Thick stateful server architecture

The server keeps the necessary state in memory of the client state of the page. In this way, when any request hits the server (usually user actions), the server sends the appropriate HTML and/or JavaScript with the concrete changes to bring the client to the new desired state (usually adding/deleting/updating a part of the client DOM). At the same time, the state in server is updated. Most of the logic is executed on the server, and HTML is usually also rendered on the server. In some ways, the server simulates a web browser, receiving events and performing delta changes in server state which are automatically propagated to client.

This approach needs more server memory and server processing, but the advantage is a simplified development model because a) the application is usually fully coded in the server, and b) data and UI state in the server are shared in the same memory space with no need for custom client/server communication bridges.

#### Thick stateless server architecture

This is a variant of the stateful server approach. The client page sends data representing its current state to the server, usually through Ajax requests. Using this data, the server is able to reconstruct the client state of the part of the page which needs to be modified and can generate the necessary data or code (for instance, as JSON or JavaScript), which is returned to the client to bring it to a new state, usually modifying the page DOM tree according to the client action that motivated the request. This approach needs less server memory and processing than the stateful server approach, but it also means that the client must handle more of the logic and state management, which can increase complexity.

These are the fundamental building blocks of any website and understanding them is crucial for anyone looking to create or maintain a website.
